<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4943400655271175561">From the bestselling author of <strong>How the Irish Saved Civilization</strong> and <strong>The Gifts of the Jews</strong>, his most compelling historical narrative yet.<br /><br />How did an obscure rabbi from a backwater of the Roman Empire come to be the central figure in Western Civilization? Did his influence in fact change the world? These are the questions Thomas Cahill addresses in his subtle and engaging inv</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4943400655271175561">From the bestselling author of <strong>How the Irish Saved Civilization</strong> and <strong>The Gifts of the Jews</strong>, his most compelling historical narrative yet.<br /><br />How did an obscure rabbi from a backwater of the Roman Empire come to be the central figure in Western Civilization? Did his influence in fact change the world? These are the questions Thomas Cahill addresses in his subtle and engaging investigation into the life and times of Jesus.<br /><br />Cahill shows us Jesus from his birth to his execution through the eyes of those who knew him and in the context of his time—a time when the Jews were struggling to maintain their beliefs under overlords who imposed their worldview on their subjects. Here is Jesus the loving friend, itinerate preacher, and quiet revolutionary, whose words and actions inspired his followers to journey throughout the Roman world and speak the truth he instilled—in the face of the greatest defeat: Jesus' crucifixion as a common criminal. Daring, provocative, and stunningly original, Cahill's interpretation will both delight and surprise.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4943400655271175561">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_77737432">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/77737432" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2025389-cv-rick" class="left imgcol" title="CV Rick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1240805325p2/2025389.jpg" alt="CV Rick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/77737432?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2025389-cv-rick" class="user" title="CV Rick">CV Rick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2025389-cv-rick?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer77737432">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6624766926243471416">It's a pleasure to read Cahill's books. Firstly he writes on transitional histories, subjects about which he's both passionate and knowledgeable. Secondly he brings those eras to life with new (to me) information and brilliant texture for the settings and the subjects. <br /><br />In Desire of the Everlasting Hills he brings the transitional event of Jesus of Nazareth to new light. In exploring the essential Jewishness of the place and of the people he shows that this man's teachings were a new doctrine and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6624766926243471416">It's a pleasure to read Cahill's books. Firstly he writes on transitional histories, subjects about which he's both passionate and knowledgeable. Secondly he brings those eras to life with new (to me) information and brilliant texture for the settings and the subjects. <br /><br />In Desire of the Everlasting Hills he brings the transitional event of Jesus of Nazareth to new light. In exploring the essential Jewishness of the place and of the people he shows that this man's teachings were a new doctrine and that he was quite the popular figure among the poor and outcast while being a threat to the establishment Jews of the day. He weaves together all the elements of life in the region and shows how those elements combined with Roman rule could have contributed to the life and death of an inspirational rabbi. Then he shows how the gospels and the new testament came to be written - all the while a new church was growing, changing, and solidifying as a world power. <br /><br />I learned quite a bit from this book and while I won't begin to tell you all of it, I will recommend it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6624766926243471416">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_572639888">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/572639888" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4762250-nathan" class="left imgcol" title="Nathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446756197p2/4762250.jpg" alt="Nathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/572639888?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 27, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4762250-nathan" class="user" title="Nathan">Nathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer572639888">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3094023242578611569">This book started out so well! It got me all excited to see how he was going to develop the way Jesus changed the landscape of western civilization, and then as soon as he started getting into the text of the Bible, it's all this liberal theology. It was pretty depressing.<br /><br />The first 65 pages are the real strength of the book. Cahill's prose is easy to read and engaging at the same time. You really get a sense that the world before Jesus was a brutal place where might makes right. It leaves you lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3094023242578611569">This book started out so well! It got me all excited to see how he was going to develop the way Jesus changed the landscape of western civilization, and then as soon as he started getting into the text of the Bible, it's all this liberal theology. It was pretty depressing.<br /><br />The first 65 pages are the real strength of the book. Cahill's prose is easy to read and engaging at the same time. You really get a sense that the world before Jesus was a brutal place where might makes right. It leaves you longing for him to show how it is that Jesus's resurrection overthrew the tragic direction of history and turned it comic. It really was excellent. I'll read it again and use it.<br /><br />The rest of the book had a few neat insights here and there. Here are two of them:<br />1. He emphasizes the grittiness of the language in the New Testament. Jesus didn't say that food is "expelled" or "eliminated" in Mark 7:19. He said that food "drops out into the s***hole."<br />2. His explanation of the book of Revelation has much to be commended in it. I think he's wrong to think John the apostle didn't write it, but his explanation is pretty good.<br /><br />The rest of the book is garbage. I don't even know where to start, it's so bad. Here's an arbitrary list:<br />1. He assumes "Q" is a real document.<br />2. He assumes most of the books of the New Testament (with the possible exception of Paul's writings) were written after the destruction of the temple (70 ad).<br />3. He doesn't think Paul wrote Ephesians. He thinks it was a woman who wrote it.<br />4. He thinks there were multiple Isaiahs.<br />5. He holds to the "moral influence" theory of the atonement.<br />6. He is very non-commital on the resurrection.<br /><br />Okay, I'll leave it at six for the symbolism. In case anybody reads this and wonders if there is an alternative, I suggest Peter Leithart's Deep Comedy. He accomplishes what Cahill is trying to do, but takes the Bible seriously, unlike Cahill. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3094023242578611569">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer932162748831033519">I'm definitely a Thomas Cahill fan. He describes Jesus Christ and his teachings into the historical setting just prior to his birth and following his death. Gave me a better understanding of the scriptures and the early days of the first believers. Details the research on the authors of the Gospels and the impact Paul had on interpreting Christ to the early followers of the "Jesus Movement". Made me want to study more carefully the teachings of Paul.<br /><br />I need to remember: "...those of us who have '</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText932162748831033519">I'm definitely a Thomas Cahill fan. He describes Jesus Christ and his teachings into the historical setting just prior to his birth and following his death. Gave me a better understanding of the scriptures and the early days of the first believers. Details the research on the authors of the Gospels and the impact Paul had on interpreting Christ to the early followers of the "Jesus Movement". Made me want to study more carefully the teachings of Paul.<br /><br />I need to remember: "...those of us who have 'died with Christ,' who have allowed themselves--at least vicariously--to experience all the depth of human suffering, can never stoop to gaining advantage over another, eve if the other is clearly in the wrong." pg 134.</span>

              <a href="/review/list/127289-emily?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/127289-emily?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1883676">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6199725713741587159">Apparently, I did read this book a long time ago. Well, I listened to it on audiobook again this week and I have new thoughts. I also liked the book more for being on CD.<br /><br />This book was recommended to me over a decade ago by an ex-boyfriend. I wouldn’t have picked it up except that I saw it in the stacks while browsing the audiobooks in the library. It is perfectly delightful to come across books this way. Most of what I read is from my long to-read list or from close friends and family’s recommen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6199725713741587159">Apparently, I did read this book a long time ago. Well, I listened to it on audiobook again this week and I have new thoughts. I also liked the book more for being on CD.<br /><br />This book was recommended to me over a decade ago by an ex-boyfriend. I wouldn’t have picked it up except that I saw it in the stacks while browsing the audiobooks in the library. It is perfectly delightful to come across books this way. Most of what I read is from my long to-read list or from close friends and family’s recommendations, but sometimes a browse will unearth wonders. On to the book itself!<br /><br />I did not agree with all of Cahill’s arguments, but most of them were plausible and all of them reflected the author’s sincere and studied thoughts about Jesus as a person, as a historical figure and as an influence in our world today. Cahill is a bit too sympathetic to Paul for my tastes, but he did convince me to love him more. Most importantly, I think, Cahill encourages the reader to recognize that Christianity could not exist without Judaism and that Jesus’ message of radical love to all people is what matters most. I don’t often “get” the necessity of Jesus’ sacrifice, but Cahill brought me as close as I’ve been in years by reminding me that Jesus as a man experienced true sympathy – suffering with us – to express his love. It makes the idea of “Emanuel” – God with us – that much deeper. Perhaps the most lasting part of the book for me will be a bit from the end, which I’ll paraphrase. A Jewish woman told the author that she had no interest whatsoever in becoming a Christian but that she loves Jesus and considers him one of her own. That’s kind of how I feel. I dropped the trappings of formal Christianity years ago, but I still love Jesus. <br /><br />I may have gotten more out of the book by reading a hardcopy or Kindle version because I probably would have slowed down, reread passages and perhaps written things down, but Brian O’Bryne’s Irish brogue was perfectly lilting, lyrical and sympathetic. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6199725713741587159">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Christians and non-Christians</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=christianity" class="actionLinkLite">christianity</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2526106-jason?shelf=religion" class="actionLinkLite">religion</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer72514306">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12042604411062190951"><blockquote>[T]his book is part of a series on cultural impact. And the great question about Jesus must always be: Did he make a difference? Is our world--in the century that began with the Turkish genocide against the Armenians, reached its nadir with the "scientific" holocaust of six million Jews (and five million others), not to speak of the slaughter by their own governments of Russians and Chinese in the scores of millions, and now comes to its end with genocides in central Africa and "ethnic cleansing</blockquote></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12042604411062190951"><blockquote>[T]his book is part of a series on cultural impact. And the great question about Jesus must always be: Did he make a difference? Is our world--in the century that began with the Turkish genocide against the Armenians, reached its nadir with the "scientific" holocaust of six million Jews (and five million others), not to speak of the slaughter by their own governments of Russians and Chinese in the scores of millions, and now comes to its end with genocides in central Africa and "ethnic cleansings" in the Balkans that are still, horribly enough "in progress"--is our world any better than the one inhabited by the Celts and Romans of twenty-four centuries ago? Did the values preached by Jesus influence the Anglican Queen Elizabeth or her opponent the Catholic Earl O'Neill? Did she ever shudder at the carnage of her battlefields? Did he, even once, as he surveyed the hacked limbs, the gouged eyes, the grisly dying, ever wonder if there was another way? Do Christian values have any influence on the actions of Christians who on both sides of the English/Irish divide have continued to "fight the old fight again"? Did the life and death of Jesus make any difference to the denizens of first-century TransTiberim? Does he make any difference to the residents of today's Trastevere?<br /><br />These are hard questions; some will no doubt label them unfair. But they must be posed at the outset. For if this Jesus, this figure professedly central to our whole culture, has had no effect, he has no place in a history of cultural effects. (8-9)</blockquote><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><blockquote>As we now stand at the entrance to the third millennium since Jesus, we can look back over the horrors of Christian history, never doubting for an instant that if Christians had put kindness ahead of devotion to good order, theological correctness, and our own justifications--if we had followed in the humble footsteps of a heretical Samaritan who was willing to wash someone else's wounds, rather than in the self-regarding steps of the priest and the immaculate steps of the levite--the world we inhabit would be a very different one. (185)</blockquote></span>

      <a href="/user/show/912433-peter" class="user" title="Peter">Peter</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/912433-peter?shelf=history" class="actionLinkLite">history</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/912433-peter?shelf=religion-faith" class="actionLinkLite">religion-faith</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer746299637">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10942094004959431221">The third Cahill book I read this summer. And like the previous book, the Gifts of the Jews, this ought to be required reading in high schools and colleges. For similar reasons as the previous book. Those these books have religion as an important component of the works, requiring them does not mean requiring belief in religion. However, our educational system falls short in ignoring the importance of religion, its importance, and what ideas that spring forth of it that sets the West apart from o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10942094004959431221">The third Cahill book I read this summer. And like the previous book, the Gifts of the Jews, this ought to be required reading in high schools and colleges. For similar reasons as the previous book. Those these books have religion as an important component of the works, requiring them does not mean requiring belief in religion. However, our educational system falls short in ignoring the importance of religion, its importance, and what ideas that spring forth of it that sets the West apart from other regions in the world. It is too readily dismissive of religion, quickly seizing on its faults and failures. We forget to discuss that those faults and failures are do to the human nature of religion; those who guide and direct it. Those who dismiss religion for its human faults, then want to turn around and enlarge the power and sphere of government, as if it is not plagued by those same problems and concerns. <br /><br />As in the previous book, Cahill puts forth criticism of Christianity, in how it develops and how it is practiced. However, he examines the story of Christ, lays out the distinctions between the four Gospels - their histories and interpretations, as well the importance of Paul in the early church. He explores some modern issues around the interpretation of Jesus, as both religion and history. With some disdain he recounts how a group in California, made up of some people of faith, some scholars, and even some movie people discuss and debate the current attempts of interpretation. He realizes there is something much stronger going on to be left to a self-selected group of elites. <br /><br />A great book, one well worth the reading. I know there is the fourth Cahill book in the series (The Hinges of History) on the imporatance of the Greeks which I have to read. He is also coming out with a new book on the Reformation and the Renaissance. Something else to look forward to reading.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_56849204">

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/56849204?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 31, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/790095-tifnie" class="user" title="Tifnie">Tifnie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tifnie by: </span>
      Shawnah
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/790095-tifnie?shelf=historical-non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer56849204">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12269650597180590004">I actually started taking notes while I was reading this book for 2 reasons. 1 - this wasn't my book and 2 - I wanted to remember some key points. There are so many points to cover - where do I start?<br /><br />Desire of the Everlasting Hills isn't about The Fate of Human Societies like I originally thought, that book will appear later on my list, it's about Judism and Christianity. More importantly, it's about discrepancies among the disciples. What one disciple heard in a crowd of people, no one else hea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12269650597180590004">I actually started taking notes while I was reading this book for 2 reasons. 1 - this wasn't my book and 2 - I wanted to remember some key points. There are so many points to cover - where do I start?<br /><br />Desire of the Everlasting Hills isn't about The Fate of Human Societies like I originally thought, that book will appear later on my list, it's about Judism and Christianity. More importantly, it's about discrepancies among the disciples. What one disciple heard in a crowd of people, no one else heard. Or better yet, an act that Jesus did that authors didn't know where it belonged. Did it belong with Luke, where it was originally, or does it belong with John, where it was later moved? But that wasn't all it was about.<br /><br />Censorship. ...for only the best reasons, of course.<br /><br />The act I'm referring to above, is when Jesus came upon a woman who committed adultery and men were about to stone her and Jesus said, "He among you who is sinless, let him cast the first stone." After all the men left without throwing one stone, Jesus told the woman to leave and avoid this sin. It was infact, excised from Luke, after which if floated arond the Christian churches, till some scribe squeezed it into the manuscript of John, where they thought it might best belong. The early church did not forgive adultery. This excision is the first recorded instance of ecclesiastical censorship.<br /><br />The Great Church quickly became far more interested in discipline &amp; order than Jesus had ever shown himself to be. <br /><br />Censorship.<br /><br />Luke's account of Jesus is based on what he believes Jesus should be. He is stoic, so all his accounts of Jesus depict that behavoir. Whereas, Peter accounts Jesus of being more emotional, more human. <br /><br />Pictures of Jesus nailed to the cross show nails going threw his palms, when in reality it is not possible, he was nailed in the wrists. Also, pictures of Jesus on the cross show him wearing a loin cloth when in reality he was naked. <br /><br />Censorship. ...for only the best reasons of course.<br /><br />The image of the crucified holds us and will not let go. In the end, it has little to do with whether we believe Jesus to have been Messiah or Son of God. Even if it explains nothing of heaven (or even of Earth), it embodies the depths of human pain.<br /><br />I took a lot from this book. But couple this book with Nietzsche's the Anti-Christ and I just shake my head. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12269650597180590004">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/4104075-kurt" class="left imgcol" title="Kurt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1299806831p2/4104075.jpg" alt="Kurt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/115053516?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 20, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4104075-kurt" class="user" title="Kurt">Kurt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4104075-kurt?shelf=20-nonfiction-in-2010" class="actionLinkLite">20-nonfiction-in-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer115053516">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2275472012289915844">Really more like 3.5 stars. This was a good book overall, definitely learned something. I really had a tough time with some of the historical research methods of identifying writing and style. It was good because it helped me think about my preconceived notions that were based on no knowledge, but I was disappointed by ideas like the assertion that certain books attributed to Paul within the text itself could not have been written by him simply because of the academic consensus was that the idea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2275472012289915844">Really more like 3.5 stars. This was a good book overall, definitely learned something. I really had a tough time with some of the historical research methods of identifying writing and style. It was good because it helped me think about my preconceived notions that were based on no knowledge, but I was disappointed by ideas like the assertion that certain books attributed to Paul within the text itself could not have been written by him simply because of the academic consensus was that the ideas in one book seem to be somewhat at odds with his other writings. I guess there was not quite enough rigor presented with these arguments for my taste. But, perhaps he is asserting something that has been rigorously examined within the appropriate academic theological literature and simply summarizing rather than arguing; if so, I missed it. Also, while his description of (the majority of) Paul's writings and the Synoptic gospels resonated with my own personal understandings of the teachings, his depiction of the Gospel of John seemed borderline outrageous relative to my experience in reading it. As someone who has only read John in it's entirety (meaning as a single, whole item rather than a chapter here and there) maybe 3 times, its possible I'm just being obtuse, but to me it seemed dangerously close to the very thing he was pointing out John's Gospel was not, which is a Gnostic Gospel. It seemed that he is asserting a much more man-created gospel sewn together of the most pious elements of the Synoptics and polished with over-wrought language. I have never, ever thought anything even remotely like that before. <br /><br />All that said, this book does an excellent job of describing the simplicity of Jesus' views on justice and love, and calls for (and presents) greater understanding of Jesus by those who claim to follow him, which is all extremely relevant to me and to my generation.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2275472012289915844">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer11432768676869940619">I've read some of Cahill's other books, and this was beyond doubt my favorite. He is clearly a believer, but not one who is afraid to examine his beliefs in the light of scientific and historical evidence. What I really loved about it, though, was his knack for highlighting the real beauty of the Bible. Many people think the Bible is boring, and to be honest some parts are (Numbers, anyone?), but it also contains more truth and beauty than anything else in western literature, and Cahill knew exa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11432768676869940619">I've read some of Cahill's other books, and this was beyond doubt my favorite. He is clearly a believer, but not one who is afraid to examine his beliefs in the light of scientific and historical evidence. What I really loved about it, though, was his knack for highlighting the real beauty of the Bible. Many people think the Bible is boring, and to be honest some parts are (Numbers, anyone?), but it also contains more truth and beauty than anything else in western literature, and Cahill knew exactly what passages to quote. Reading them here was like reading them for the first time, and I seemed to feel something of the excitement and joy of the very first Christians. Highly recommended </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1044339933">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1044339933" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20486322-brenda" class="left imgcol" title="Brenda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1370708951p2/20486322.jpg" alt="Brenda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1044339933?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 03, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20486322-brenda" class="user" title="Brenda">Brenda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1044339933">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6036468426550239868">On this Good Friday in 2014 it seems appropriate to consider the impact of Jesus on our history, culture and thought. Thomas Cahill in his continuing series on the Hinges of History takes up the question of Jesus and his impact on Western history and thought in The Desire of the Everlasting Hills, the third book in this projected 6-7 volume series. For Cahill, this series is a means to explore the stories of the “great gift givers who throughout history entrusted to our keeping one or another of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6036468426550239868">On this Good Friday in 2014 it seems appropriate to consider the impact of Jesus on our history, culture and thought. Thomas Cahill in his continuing series on the Hinges of History takes up the question of Jesus and his impact on Western history and thought in The Desire of the Everlasting Hills, the third book in this projected 6-7 volume series. For Cahill, this series is a means to explore the stories of the “great gift givers who throughout history entrusted to our keeping one or another of the singular treasures that make up the patrimony of the West.”<br /><br />Cahill examines Jesus through the history and culture of his time and through the Gospels and letters that were written after Jesus death and shows us through their eyes the man that they knew and followed.<br /><br />Jesus was born in a time where it was assumed and a given that war brought peace. The history of the Jews up until that time bore that out, with the conquered peoples like the Jews having to pay the price of the conquerors. And yet even among the conquerors there was a deep desire for a peace that that would be sustained and not broken by more war. As Virgil wrote of his hope in his Fourth Eclogue, ” Now is a child engendered by heaven….who will put an end to our wretched age…the herds will have no fear of lions…the serpent will be no more,” there was a deep and abiding hope “..not for an emperor, not for a Exalted One-but for a Just One” who would heal the brokenness they saw all around them..<br /><br />Yet, Jesus’ message was simple and as can be seen in the Beatitudes the message was to two audiences: “the powerless that need to be reminded that God loves them and will see to their ultimate triumph and the powerful who need to be goaded by the example of those who have abandoned their comfort for the sake of others.” As Cahill says, Jesus’s Good News or Gospel was to “comfort the afflicted and to afflict the comforted.” And in his death on the cross Jesus gave us that ultimate act of sympathy; a promise that he knows and understands our pain, our sorrow, and with<br /><br />his death promises “I will suffer with you.”<br /><br />As always Cahill is informative, engaging and stimulating. I, as a practicing Christian may have more interest than most in Jesus and his impact on western thought and culture, but I was reminded again at how grounded in the Jewish tradition Christianity is, and as Cahill quotes Rabbi Shaye Cohen who says “…that Christianity too, is (or at least once was) a form of Judaism,” and regained a sense of the gift that tradition is to my own faith journey.<br /><br />Cahill’s concise and interesting reflections on the various gospel writers and his explication of their messages was extremely helpful in seeing the message of the early church as a whole. Cahill weaves the history art,and poetry of that time period throughout his analysis of the Gospels which provides a fascinating and grounded view of the life of the early Church. This is definitely a feast to be savored and read slowly.<br /><br />Brenda’s Rating: *****(5 Stars out 5)<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6036468426550239868">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/862094725?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 22, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5905485-casey-carr" class="user" title="Casey Carr">Casey Carr</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer862094725">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17666058832460357147">Excellent book, dense as these books tend to be bet a very good presentation of the story of Jesus. Mr Cahill does what many historians do which is give more credence to the historical and "factual" nature of religious texts, which simply cannot be known and is dubious at best, and he sets up a lot of false choices ("Either there was something about these experiences that left the minds of the recipients clouded as to time and circumstance, or the experiences themselves were of such a timeless n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17666058832460357147">Excellent book, dense as these books tend to be bet a very good presentation of the story of Jesus. Mr Cahill does what many historians do which is give more credence to the historical and "factual" nature of religious texts, which simply cannot be known and is dubious at best, and he sets up a lot of false choices ("Either there was something about these experiences that left the minds of the recipients clouded as to time and circumstance, or the experiences themselves were of such a timeless nature that it seemed impossible to insert them into a normal consecutive chronology" pg 221).<br /><br />Reading as an agnostic/atheist, this book shifted my perspective on Christianity and it's place among the world's religions. It hasn't made a believer out of me, but it confirmed (for me at least) the feeling that I have long held that Christianity is a worthy philosophy, minus some unredeemable elements, and that it has always been corrupted by church and religion and basic human prejudice. That the core of it's teaching and the only thing worth taking away for all humanity is to love thy neighbor as thyself. Cahill presents this as a solely Christian sentiment, something which I very much disagree with. He also presents Christianity as the foundation of Democracy and it's defining documents, the United States Declaration of Independence and Constitution, without so much as a nod to enlightenment philosophy, which I believe played a much greater role in Western Democracy as we know it today, but even if he disagrees, it at least deserves a mention....<br /><br />Mr Cahill writes from the perspective of a believer and as long as I take that into consideration, the things that I see as lapses in a properly honest &amp; unbiased account do not spoil for me the richness of the story and his take on it, extremely well informed and sourced. Highly recommend.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_989031915">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/989031915" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/20566510-a" class="left imgcol" title="A."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1385694465p2/20566510.jpg" alt="A." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/989031915?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 11, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/20566510-a" class="user" title="A.">A.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/20566510-a?shelf=essays" class="actionLinkLite">essays</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer989031915">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17724983447684011015">Review: Cahill, Thomas. <em>How the Irish Saved Civilization, The Gifts of the Jews, Sailing the Wine Dark Sea, Desire of the Everlasting Hills, Heretics and Heroes</em><br /><br /> The Hinges of History is a series including the above books plus Mysteries of the Middle Ages and a volume yet to be published. I am treating them together because, as one might expect, they share many strengths and weaknesses of the author, Thomas Cahill.<br /> Heretics and Heroes was the first book I read, it being a gift, and, therefore, re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17724983447684011015">Review: Cahill, Thomas. <em>How the Irish Saved Civilization, The Gifts of the Jews, Sailing the Wine Dark Sea, Desire of the Everlasting Hills, Heretics and Heroes</em><br /><br />	The Hinges of History is a series including the above books plus Mysteries of the Middle Ages and a volume yet to be published. I am treating them together because, as one might expect, they share many strengths and weaknesses of the author, Thomas Cahill.<br />	Heretics and Heroes was the first book I read, it being a gift, and, therefore, required reading. It covers the period including the Reformation and the Counter-Reformation, a period with which I had nodding acquaintance. I was overwhelmed at the apparent scholarship of the author. I especially appreciated the discussions of art, a subject with which I am fairly ignorant. The writing is superb: it reads like a novel. Inspired by it, I read a couple of books on the period and its persons, especially Erasmus, all to my benefit, but not necessarily to the benefit of Thomas Cahill. However, I enjoyed Heroes so much that I determined to read the others in the series.<br />	Next I read How the Irish Saved Civilization, a decidedly less engaging work. Having some knowledge of the subject, I found this slender work very enjoyable—Cahill writes very well—but unconvincing. The copious quotations recalled to mind the copious quotations in Heroes. He did not prove his point to me, nor did he make a plausible case for salvific Irish.<br />	The Gifts of the Jews continued the bravura elegance and style of Cahill, but, having substantial knowledge of Jewish history, religion, and customs, I found the work lacking. Much of the work comprises quotations from the Hebrew Bible, with extrapolations of the text into possible meanings, but without meaningful comment on the validity of the quoted text: where did it come from? how was it developed? when was it written? by whom? what were its sources? Further, apparent contradictions in extrapolated meanings were not discussed.<br />	Sailing the Wine Dark Sea had a distinct advantage over How the Irish Saved Civilization and The Gifts of the Jews: the now-expected extensive quotations reflected elegant Greek writers who had something to say. Again, Cahill’s filler flashed the style and brilliance I had come to expect from him. He, correctly, let them speak.<br />	 The Desire of the Everlasting Hills disappointed me in the extreme, probably because I know this subject very well. I kept making marginal notes as to the unfounded conclusions of the author based upon a text not validated by common sense historical standards, all the time admiring his use of very extensive quotations and brilliant style as superb technique. (After all, I read five of his works.) The deficiency of this work will be apparent to anyone who has read the New Testament seriously enough to consult scholarly opinion which may differ from personally held beliefs.<br />	I cannot recommend Cahill’s books highly enough for elegance, style, and entrepreneurship. Cahill is a storyteller par excellence. Storytellers, however, long ago evolved into historians, a breed which developed a formal discipline rigorous enough to characterize it as a science. This may be a loss to literature, but not to science. Read Cahill as a storyteller. You will very likely enjoy his writing.<br /><br />Mr. Graziano is the author of <em>From the Cross to the Church: the Emergence of the Church from the Chaos of the Crucifixion.</em><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17724983447684011015">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1136195143">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1706380330990024985">It gets an extra star for being readable, but one star in terms of its ostensible focus. The birth of Christianity is certainly an appropriate subject for a series that bills itself as the "Hinges of History", but, aside from a little bit of historical context-setting on the subject of Alexander and the Hellenization of the Jewish lands, this book is decidedly <em>not</em> "the world before and after Jesus". It's a completely uncritical take on the stories advanced in the four canonical Gospels and the b</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1706380330990024985">It gets an extra star for being readable, but one star in terms of its ostensible focus. The birth of Christianity is certainly an appropriate subject for a series that bills itself as the "Hinges of History", but, aside from a little bit of historical context-setting on the subject of Alexander and the Hellenization of the Jewish lands, this book is decidedly <em>not</em> "the world before and after Jesus". It's a completely uncritical take on the stories advanced in the four canonical Gospels and the book of Acts. Cahill ignores the fact that these accounts are inconsistent in many respects and strike false notes (one he should <em>definitely</em> be aware of is the fact that, contrary to Luke's infancy narrative, non-citizens at this time weren't taxed, but subject to tribute, which applied to the whole country under subjection and not any one individual) that might lead in interesting directions. By the time he finally gets around to admitting that Jesus wasn't originally believed to have been the sole <em>begotten</em> son of God, that fact is just dropped in almost as an aside, and no investigation is made into how we got from the beliefs of an early offshoot of Judaism to the formal, ossified theology of the Catholic and Orthodox churches. If you were able to halt the historical development of Christianity at the point where Cahill stops dealing with it—at the level of a few hundred to a few thousand followers in small household churches—then Christianity would have died out with hardly a mark left to note its passing, and it certainly wouldn't have gained the sway that makes it an important subject to tackle in a history series on Western civilization.<br /><br />Cahill stumps for the earliest plausible datings of Gospels, insists on their being the result of eyewitness testimony, and elides the contradictions and historical inaccuracies. He even accepts the authenticity of the Shroud of Turin! He admits that it's been carbon dated to the medieval era, but claims that organic contamination could throw off the date. In reality, you'd need several centimeters of crusted organic material to throw the dating off by approximately 1300 years and it would be obvious to everyone. Aside from the carbon dating, there's also the slight problem that it cannot be caused by any human body. Assuming the shroud is supposed to have been folded over the head of Jesus, then Jesus must have been two-dimensional because the shroud image lines up the back of the head to the front of the head, and omits the top of the head entirely!<br /><br />I really don't know for whom this book is intended. Christians can simply read the four Gospels without Cahill's retelling and non-Christians are going to be put off by his transparent cheerleading and his superficiality. I do not exaggerate when I say that the only use I can conceive of for this book is to help script your next Christmas pageant.<br /><br />I recommend <em>
  <a title="Pagans and Christians by Robin Lane Fox" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/665147.Pagans_and_Christians">Pagans and Christians: in the Mediterranean world from the second century AD to the conversion of Constantine</a>
</em> by <a title="Robin Lane Fox" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/14636.Robin_Lane_Fox">Robin Lane Fox</a> as a far superior treatment of the subject and for being more like what this book promised to be and wasn't.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1706380330990024985">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3615313843961551624">Thomas Cahill set out to answer the question of the actual significance of Christ on Western Civilization. He also wished to acquaint readers with the Jewishness of Jesus as well as the Jewishness of the synoptic gospels, an often significant oversight by anyone who wishes to understand the development of what is known today as Christianity. The subject matter (a mere two thousand years of history) is complex and its scope is truly epic. Thomas is to be commended for making it very accessible to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3615313843961551624">Thomas Cahill set out to answer the question of the actual significance of Christ on Western Civilization. He also wished to acquaint readers with the Jewishness of Jesus as well as the Jewishness of the synoptic gospels, an often significant oversight by anyone who wishes to understand the development of what is known today as Christianity. The subject matter (a mere two thousand years of history) is complex and its scope is truly epic. Thomas is to be commended for making it very accessible to the non-theolgian and non-historian alike. He draws on rich sources for his history of the Christian Age, and the age preceding and following it, and he explains why he favored one work over another where the perspectives on such issues as the claims for Christ's messianic title or authorship of the synoptic gospels (to name two) differ widely. His passion for his subject matter shines through his work as well as his deep love for humanity, our struggle for understanding and transcendence. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16384161904058035709">Thomas Cahill is a fine author. His holistic approach to history/theology/mythology examines the transitions of human consciousness. In this third installment of his series, he focuses on the Jesus movement and what it has brought to the world.<br /><br />I found this to be a worthwhile experience.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText16384161904058035709">Thomas Cahill is a fine author. His holistic approach to history/theology/mythology examines the transitions of human consciousness. In this third installment of his series, he focuses on the Jesus movement and what it has brought to the world.<br /><br />I found this to be a worthwhile experience. Cahill balances the micro -- with an examination of the life and times of Jesus -- and the macro -- with his overall thesis on how a short moment in history changed the very trajectory of mankind forever.<br /><br />Not only did this study serve my intellectual curiosity but my spiritual inquisitions as well ... a very rare synthesis in our binary world of reason vs devotion.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1238636270">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1238636270">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3140403490896263666">Excellent book. He begins by describing the political/religious setting during the conquest of Alexander and then the Maccabean revolt. He then follows through to the times of Jesus and the Hasmonean dynasty, including Herod the Great and his sons. Then, he takes each Gospel and discusses how each Gospel focuses on Jesus a little differently, Matthew: Jewish, Luke: Gentile, Mark: most ancient Gospel and focuses on quick facts, John: High Christology and distance from Jews. He also includes a fas</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3140403490896263666">Excellent book. He begins by describing the political/religious setting during the conquest of Alexander and then the Maccabean revolt. He then follows through to the times of Jesus and the Hasmonean dynasty, including Herod the Great and his sons. Then, he takes each Gospel and discusses how each Gospel focuses on Jesus a little differently, Matthew: Jewish, Luke: Gentile, Mark: most ancient Gospel and focuses on quick facts, John: High Christology and distance from Jews. He also includes a fascinating insight to Paul and his cosmic Christ. Overall, a great read for anyone looking to discover more about the ancient story of Jesus and the climate which He lived and died.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_856372773">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/856372773" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/18281014-don-camp" class="left imgcol" title="Don Camp"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1363062046p2/18281014.jpg" alt="Don Camp" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/856372773?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 09, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18281014-don-camp" class="user" title="Don Camp">Don Camp</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer856372773">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16036446734789522661">I enjoyed the combination of poetic prose and scholarship of Cahill. His scholarship, in particular, is impressive. Regarding the content, Cahill provides a very readable and respectful version of modern scholarship's interpretation Jesus. Cahill also provides a good review of the impact Jesus and the movement that followed had upon world history, ending with a wonderful description of the Trastevere community in Rome. (The latter is a great model for all who want to develop a missional church t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16036446734789522661">I enjoyed the combination of poetic prose and scholarship of Cahill. His scholarship, in particular, is impressive. Regarding the content, Cahill provides a very readable and respectful version of modern scholarship's interpretation Jesus. Cahill also provides a good review of the impact Jesus and the movement that followed had upon world history, ending with a wonderful description of the Trastevere community in Rome. (The latter is a great model for all who want to develop a missional church today.)<br /><br />Though I enjoyed the book and found it thought provoking, I can't, however, fully recommend it. I feel Cahill misses the point too often when it comes to the nature of Jesus and the nature of the New Testament documents. Regarding the documents, his approach is humanistic. He regards the writings as purely the work of men and controlled entirely by the context from which they arose. There seems no room for inspiration in the sense that Evangelicals would use that word. <br /><br />Regarding Jesus, Cahill's dismissal of John's gospel as being the interpretation of a late 1st century author (and then deeply edited by a later editor) eliminates the idea that Jesus was the Son of God in any sense that Evangelicals would use that word today and understand it in the context of the Gospel of John. Simply put, for Cahill Jesus is not divine. <br /><br />That begs the question of how Jesus and the movement that grew from his memory came to change the world so radically. Cahill's answer is that "Anyone on earth could do what they (the Trastevere community) have done," and, I would infer, that Christians have done. I think that conclusion seriously lacking rational. <br /><br />So read with your mind in gear. If you do, you'll find the read worthwhile.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16036446734789522661">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/759038744?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 01, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/25389863-david-donnelly" class="user" title="David Donnelly">David Donnelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer759038744">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14321028171433192478">Cahill's Hinges of History series of works chart the development of Christianity from its Mesopotamian roots through the renaissance with his latest volume. The point of view is decidedly literary and intellectual and will appeal most directly to the educated Catholic. Marketing of How the Irish Saved Civilization, etc. creates some dissonance when one encounters the material for the first time as it is not what one expects. After reading the entire corpus of work I have begun to appreciate Cahi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14321028171433192478">Cahill's Hinges of History series of works chart the development of Christianity from its Mesopotamian roots through the renaissance with his latest volume. The point of view is decidedly literary and intellectual and will appeal most directly to the educated Catholic. Marketing of How the Irish Saved Civilization, etc. creates some dissonance when one encounters the material for the first time as it is not what one expects. After reading the entire corpus of work I have begun to appreciate Cahill's unique ability to synthesize the various source materials and draw our attention to vignettes that illustrate the slowly constructed theme. The Desire of the Everlasting Hills now appears dated in spots because of contemporary references but it manages to encapsulate most of New Testament Scholarship. As with all his works, carefully annotated bibliographies provide entree into further detailed reading for those interested. There is certainly an ecumenical point of view at work, Christianity providing the reference point for the development of Western Civilization no less. Well worth the time invested.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14321028171433192478">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_68715959">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68715959" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1797158-matt" class="left imgcol" title="Matt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1334325752p2/1797158.jpg" alt="Matt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/68715959?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1797158-matt" class="user" title="Matt">Matt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer68715959">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14622958887727727502">Cahill's history writing doesn't make the cut among scholars, probably for the same reason that reading him is so enjoyable: he is a good storyteller in a discipline sorely lacking them. Nevertheless, this book left me wanting. After an interesting but cursory glance at the world and value systems that preceded Jesus, Cahill approaches this mysterious figure through the lenses of the principle gospel authors. It's a good read for anyone wanting an introduction to the gospels, but Cahill's messag</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14622958887727727502">Cahill's history writing doesn't make the cut among scholars, probably for the same reason that reading him is so enjoyable: he is a good storyteller in a discipline sorely lacking them. Nevertheless, this book left me wanting. After an interesting but cursory glance at the world and value systems that preceded Jesus, Cahill approaches this mysterious figure through the lenses of the principle gospel authors. It's a good read for anyone wanting an introduction to the gospels, but Cahill's message is quaint and even ham-handed: Jesus focused far more on taking care of the poor and needy than on religious procedure, even "casually forgiving" sexual sins. True enough, but how many times must that be repeated, almost desperately, by Jesus apologists? <br /><br />One of Cahill's more interesting observations is that disciples of the Jesus Movement, as he calls it, couldn't bring themselves to depict the crucifixion in art until the third century. Their messiah had died in the most shameful, destructively humiliating way imaginable. It reminds me of Paul Fryer's poignant, excellent, controversial (and inaccurately named) "Pieta" in which Jesus is portrayed dead from the electric chair. <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://latimesblogs.latimes.com/culturemonster/2009/04/oh-the-french-cathedral-displays-sculpture-of-jesus-christ-in-the-electric-chair.html" target="_blank">http://latimesblogs.latimes.com/cultu...</a> <br /><br />As usual with these types of books, the faithful will find truth in Cahill's reassuring prose yet skeptics will remain unsatisfied. But perhaps that's because there isn't much left to say about the elusive superstar from a backwater of the Roman Empire.</span>

      <a href="/user/show/5941368-kerry-hennigan" class="user" title="Kerry Hennigan">Kerry Hennigan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer720037488">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10413260767835977684">One of the volumes of Thomas Cahill's series The Hinges of History, Desire of the Everlasting Hills tells the story of the Jewish world of the Middle East before the coming of Christianity and of a young Jew named Jesus whose philosophy gave rise to groups of followers who became the first members of a new world faith.<br /><br />It is a violent world, a world of Roman overlords and the Jewish leaders who accommodate them. It is also a world or zealots and revolutionaries. Jesus was one of the latter, it ha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10413260767835977684">One of the volumes of Thomas Cahill's series The Hinges of History, Desire of the Everlasting Hills tells the story of the Jewish world of the Middle East before the coming of Christianity and of a young Jew named Jesus whose philosophy gave rise to groups of followers who became the first members of a new world faith.<br /><br />It is a violent world, a world of Roman overlords and the Jewish leaders who accommodate them. It is also a world or zealots and revolutionaries. Jesus was one of the latter, it has been said, but his was a revolution of the spirit; but not everyone understood that.<br /><br />Religious studies in school – even a Catholic school where there was an emphasis on it – were never this thorough when I was a kid. Cahill looks at the philosophical influences that preceded Jesus, as well as the changes that came after his death.<br /><br />How did small bands of clandestine followers, hiding from the Jewish hierarchy and the Romans, give rise to what became the Catholic church, which, in the centuries after Emperor Constantine who legalized Christianity, came to dominate the corridors of power with its influence over kings and emperors.<br /><br />This is not a book to change your beliefs. Rather it is a story of the rise of a world movement that is still with us today, and still extremely powerful and influential. Understanding what it was at the beginning should, if anything, strengthen one’s appreciation for the message of the wandering preacher and his eclectic band of followers, both male and female.<br /><br />Review by Kerry Hennigan<br />October 2013</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10413260767835977684">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_27699910">

  <a href="/user/show/1340353-terry" class="left imgcol" title="terry"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1339459034p2/1340353.jpg" alt="terry" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27699910?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 19, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1340353-terry" class="user" title="terry">terry</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27699910">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5494083128290762988">Cahill's writing style is as accessible and engaging and his scholarship is vast. I especially like his use of small margin notes with additional thought, translation, history, commentary. Here is a sample of his language related to Jesus's baptism scene, which has led me to think of it as a compelling view for adoptive christology: <br /><br />As he breaks the surface of the water, he sees the heavens torn open and God's Spirit "like a dove, descending..." Mark fails to tell us if anyone but Jesus saw and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5494083128290762988">Cahill's writing style is as accessible and engaging and his scholarship is vast. I especially like his use of small margin notes with additional thought, translation, history, commentary. Here is a sample of his language related to Jesus's baptism scene, which has led me to think of it as a compelling view for adoptive christology: <br /><br />As he breaks the surface of the water, he sees the heavens torn open and God's Spirit "like a dove, descending..." Mark fails to tell us if anyone but Jesus saw and heard these marvels. But there can be no question that this moment is central to Jesus's life. Like all the prophets before him, he has received the Spirit, lamentably absent during the preceding prophet-less centuries. Henceforth, the words that come from his mouth, like those of Isiah and all of Jesus's prophetic predecessors, will be the words of God himself. This is a difficult scene for modern readers to take seriously. Despite the fact that many of us secretly harbor uncanny experiences that have helped us lead our lives, the tenor of our age encourages us to brush these off as "coincidences" and we don't talk about such things lest everyone think we're nuts. Instead, we--publicly, at least--relegate all such experiences to the dustbin of pathology and speak dismissively of narcissism, Messiah complexes, and delusions of grandeur. The trouble with this response is that it fails to explain the rest of Jesus's life...<br /><br /></span>

  <a href="/user/show/1336101-joan" class="left imgcol" title="Joan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301285503p2/1336101.jpg" alt="Joan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/179349853?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 07, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1336101-joan" class="user" title="Joan">Joan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who appreciate history and its  influence on culture</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer179349853">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11594917187085439063">This is, as one my friends says, ". . . my kind of book!" Cahill has the writing style to really enhance history and he has definitely done this in "Desire of the Everlasting Hills." It is a beautiful, lyrical discussion of the eras just before, during, and after the time Jesus walked the hills and countryside--including Jerusalem--of Israel.<br />Cahill starts with the era of Alexander the Great, his conquests and influence on the lands he conquers. To see the world being prepared--literally--for the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11594917187085439063">This is, as one my friends says, ". . . my kind of book!" Cahill has the writing style to really enhance history and he has definitely done this in "Desire of the Everlasting Hills." It is a beautiful, lyrical discussion of the eras just before, during, and after the time Jesus walked the hills and countryside--including Jerusalem--of Israel.<br />Cahill starts with the era of Alexander the Great, his conquests and influence on the lands he conquers. To see the world being prepared--literally--for the birth of Christ is fascinating. If the people of Palestine and Greece and Syria only knew what was coming, would they be ready for the experience of hearing and seeing Him preach, teach, and heal? Who knows. But Cahill describes not just the country but also the people, the rulers, and the cultures. And he does it so lyrically!<br />I loved this book. Perhaps it takes an historian or at least someone who appreciates the role of history in culture to appreciate the book. Others in the book club we're reading it for have already expressed disappointment in the book and its contents. We will see in two days. I plan to serve fruits, falafal, hummus, and other Palestinian delecacies to the members as we discuss the book. Maybe the food will help to warm their hearts to the real heart of the book: the person of Jesus as seen through the eyes of those who lived in that world.<br />This is not a novel, not fiction. Cahill's Christian background is sort of apparent. But he is a gifted writer and a thorough historian. The book is part of his "Hinges of History" series which includes two books on Israel and the Jews and one on Ireland. Worth looking at!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11594917187085439063">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_605840476">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/605840476" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen" class="left imgcol" title="Kathleen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1365166675p2/13954899.jpg" alt="Kathleen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/605840476?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 03, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13954899-kathleen" class="user" title="Kathleen">Kathleen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13954899-kathleen?shelf=history-quasi" class="actionLinkLite">history-quasi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer605840476">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15526842359809660463">This is book #3 in the <em>Hinges of History</em> series. It's fairly interesting and thought-provoking at times. Cahill describes how the message of Christ changed civilization. He attributes to Christ (and to Christians) the gradual propagation of widespread principles of mercy, forgiveness, and second chances (opposed to the eye-for-an-eye system of retribution). He also attributes to Christianity the eventual spread of literacy and bringing an end to human sacrifice in some cultures. Cahill was a lit</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15526842359809660463">This is book #3 in the <em>Hinges of History</em> series. It's fairly interesting and thought-provoking at times. Cahill describes how the message of Christ changed civilization. He attributes to Christ (and to Christians) the gradual propagation of widespread principles of mercy, forgiveness, and second chances (opposed to the eye-for-an-eye system of retribution). He also attributes to Christianity the eventual spread of literacy and bringing an end to human sacrifice in some cultures. Cahill was a little scattered in his arguments, and is probably biased. His writing felt weak at times, yet he makes some good points.<br /><br />I read the first four books in the <em>Hinges of History</em> series, starting book 1 almost 20 years ago. They stuck with me fairly well. Since then, Cahill wrote two more books, but I have not read them. This is not fiction, but rather quasi-history told in an accessible narrative style. Each book examines how a particular European people changed the world (sorry Asians and Africans). The four cultures (one per book): Irish, Jews, Christians, Greeks. I enjoyed them all. They are easy to read. Not a historian, so cannot adequately argue Cahill's points. He probably stretched the "story" to make a strong case for the particular "gifts" he suggests the culture brought to the world, but I always read history through a strainer.<br /><br />I cannot recall whether Cahill included the contributions women made. I think not.<br /><br />The most memorable book in the Hinges of History series is #1, <a title="How the Irish Saved Civilization by Thomas Cahill" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25669.How_the_Irish_Saved_Civilization">How the Irish Saved Civilization</a>. It's set in the Dark Ages, after Rome fell, when Visigoths, Goths, and Vandals plundered, burning books, libraries, monasteries, etc. I found it riveting, but doubtless there are some holes in his argument that Irish monks "saved civilization" by saving ancient classical writings from extinction (by burning). They did this by copying ancient Greek and Latin texts (Ptolmy, Euclid, Cicero, Plato, etc), as well as ancient Biblical scriptures, creating illustrated manuscripts. A stretch to accommodate his grand theory, but nonetheless interesting.<br /><br />According to <em>The Gift of the Jews</em> (book #2), the Hebrew people introduced various key concepts to Western Civ: hygiene, the written word (along with Phonecians, Greeks, Sumarians, etc), a code of law, and monotheism, including caring for widows and orphans via a tithing system -- much like paying taxes. That's all I remember. <br /><br />Book #4 is <em>Sailing the Wine Dark Sea</em>. I enjoyed that book, but was a little disappointed. When I think of all the Greeks were and did, and how much they influenced modern civilization, I grow almost dizzy. So I was giddily anticipating the book, but it fell short of expectation. However, I was intrigued by the notion of the Greeks as intellectual scavengers, sailing the Mediterranean to various ports, bringing the best ideas and inventions back to Athens and integrating them into the Greek culture. Eventually, these ideas trickled or gushed into other cultures, and remain part of civilization today. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15526842359809660463">...more</a>

          </span>
